(i)
BURUNDI
POWER TRANSMISSION AND DISTRIBUTION PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower:                The Government of the Republic of Burundi
Amount:                  IDA  Credit  SDR  12.9  milllon  (US$12.3  million
equivalent)
Beneficiary:             R6gie  de  distribution  d'eau  et  d'Electricitd
(REGIDESO)
Terms:                   Standard
Relending Terms:         The Government will relend US$12.0 equivalent of
the Credit proceeds to REGIDESO at an interest
rate of 9.29 percent, with a 20 years repayment
period, including five years' grace. REGIDESO will
bear the foreign exchange risk.
Cofinanciers:            Belgian Government (US$1.5 million)
Caisse   Centrale   de   Cooperation   Economique
(US$5 million)
Federal Republic of Germany (US$0.8 million)
Cofinanciers' on-lending terms have not yet been
decided but are assumed to be for 20 years
including 5 years grace at an interest rate of 8X.
Project Description:    The project would augment and improve reliability
of  electricIty  supply  to  Bujumbura  and  its
environs, providing transmission and distribution
facilitites to receive additional power from Ruzizi
II, serving additional consumers in the general
context of sustaining and improving growth in the
industrial, commercial and domestic sectors of the
country. In addition, the provision of management
assistance to REGIDESO, establishment of a trainiag
center and the training of REGIDESO staff, would
result   in   improved   service   and   financial
performance   by   the   company   through   better
operating, financial and maintenance practices and
strengthening  of investment planning capability.
The service of experts provided to the Government
would serve to import petroleum products at least
cost and to sustain through 1987 and 1988 the
dissemination of improved charcoal cooking stoves
and related energy-saving techniques.



(ii)
The   project  would  include:   (a)  a   110  kV
transmission  line  (83  km)  from  Ruzizi  II  to
Bubanza; (b) construction of 110/30 kV substations
in Bubanza and Cibitoke, two 30 kV subtransmission
lines (42 km) to supply four regional towns, a 15
or 30 kV feeder to Katumba and distribution
networks  in  these towns;  (c)  construction    of
distribution   networks   to   consumers   to   six
low-income   districts   of  Bujumbura;   and   (d)
consulting   engineering   services,   management
assistance to REGIDESO, establishment of a training
center and technical assistance for the training of
REGIDESO  staff;  and  (e)  continuation  of  the
dissemination program of improved charcoal stoves
in Bujumbura and funding for the services of an
advisor on petroleum matters.
Benefits and Risks:      The  principal  benefits  of  the proposed project
would be Burundi's access to the least-cost source
of electricity and extension of the electricity
supply to the hitherto unserved population.   The
project faces no special risk.

BURUNDI
POWER TRANSMISSION AND DISTRIBUTION PROJECT
I. TIE ENERGY SECMDR
Background
1.01       Burundi is a small, landlocked country lying just south of the
equator in Eastern Central Africa. It is bordered on the north by Iwanda,
on the west by Zaire, and on the east and south by Tanzania (Map IBRD
18517). It has an area of 27,820 ku2 I/ and the 1984 estimated population
of about 4.5   million  results  in a population  density  of about 160
inhabitants per ks2, but reaches over 350 ln some prLiarily agricultural
areas, high in comparison to other African countries.  Between 1978 and
1983 the estimated annual rate of growth of the populatLon was 2.2Z, and
this ls the country's most pressing problem. About ninety-five percent of
the population lives in isolated farms in rural areas, engaged in
subsistence agriculture and all-scale coffee cultivation.  About 200,000
people (93Z of the urban population) live in Bujumbura, the capital city,
located on Lake Tanganyika. The GNP in Burundi was about US$240 per capita
in 1983.
1.02            Burundi's principal energy resource Is its forest, which is
being  rapidly  depleted  for  agricultural  clearing,  firewood  and  its
by-product, charcoal.   Peat is available in relative abundance and is a
potential substitute for fuelwood but is not traditionally used and is at
present difficult and costly both to exploit and us. The potential for
development of hydroelectrLc power exists but development is costly due to
the sites having low capacity and being widely scattered. The beat hydro
sources  are  on  the  Ruzizi  and  1i tege  rlvers; two schemaes are being
developed: one project on the river Razlzi  (para. 2.10) to meet  the
regional demand (Burundi, Rwnda and livu, Zaire) and one project
(Rwegura-para. 2.09) is  under construcLLon for BurundLan demand.   The
gravity and aeromagnetic surveys and ieisuic progras carried out to date
suggest that oil may have accumulated in exploltable quantities under Lake
Tanganyika and the Ruzizi plain, and the Government has signed in October
1984 an exploration agreement with a multinational oil company (para.
1.23). In the renewable energy subsector, reconnaissance work carried out
in 1982 on 14 geothermal sites indicated that the potential is limlted.
Some research work has been done on solar and wind energy, and biogas, but
these energy sources are unlikely to make a significant contribution in the
short to medium term.
1.03      As part of the Bank's increased activities in the energy sector
of developing countries, in April and May 1981, the Bank conducted an
energy assessment under the joint UNDP/World Bank Energy Assessment
Programme in Burundi which resulted in various recommendations in the
forestry/fuelwood, peat, petroleum, electricity and renewable energy
subsectors.   2/  Excluding  recoamendations  for  the  power  subsector
(para. 2.29), principally these were:
1/ Total land area is about 26,000 km2.
7/ -Burundi: Issues and Options in the Energy Sector", June, 1982.



-2-
(a) to slow the trend of depletion of woodfuel through extensive
planting of trees, encouragiug couservatiou measures iL promoting
better charcoal kills, wood and charcoal stoves, aud training of
extension workers;
(b) to reduce tine cost of importing oil, through direct purchase of
petroleum products from Middle East refineries and reduce the
cost of transportation by use of the Tanzauian route;
(c) to develop peat reserves and a peat market, maiLly among
potential industrial customers; and
(d) to reiuforce energy planning capability at sub-sector level.
1.04         The Goverument has been generally responsive to the Bank's
recommendations.   A UNDP Round Table discussion on energy issues and
projects, establishment of a hydrocarbon unit at the Ministry of Public
Works, Entergy and Mines (tPWLM), and an active interest in charcoal
production have followed. A status report on energy sector activities was
prepared in January 1984 following a UNDP/World Bank Energy Sector
Management Assistance Program  (ESMAP) missiou to Burundi in August 1983.
Technical assistance under ESMAP was provided to help the Government
prepare documentation for the Round Table meeting held in February 1984, to
review the urban stove improvement program; and, in the petroleum
subsector, for the interpretation of gravity and aeromagnetic surveys made
in northern Burutndi; for assessmet of the economics and reliability of the
alternative route via Tanzania for the importation of petroleum products;
and to help the Government in Its negotiations with an international
company for exploration licensilig.
1.05       A table showitg the demand for electrical and petroleum energy
for the period 1976-1983 may be found in Annex 1. Apart from biomass fuels
(fuelwood, charcoal, peat, agricultural residues), which account for about
95% of total energy needs, 752 of Burundi's remainiug energy needs are met
by petroleum products, while electricity accounts for about 23 percent and
industrially used peat about 2 percent.   The overall growth in energy
consumption (excluding wood and charcoal) duriug the 1973-1980 pariod
averaged  about  9Z  per  year.    Consumption  of  petroleum  fuels  and
electricity is equivalent to 15 kgoe/year per capita and per unit GNP Is
very low and is only about one-third of the average for other countries in
size aid income level. This reflects, inter alia, the extremely high cost
of petroleum in land-locked Burundi, aid the very low level of industrial
and tran.s"ort development.
Energy Sector Orgauization
1.06        The MPWEt  has overall responsibility for policy formulation in
the energy sector.  The main parastatal organization in the sector under
the MPWEJ4 is Regie de distributiot d'eau et d'electricit& (REGIDESO). The
Natioual Peat Organization (ONATOUR) and the D&partemeut Urbai de
Bujumbura (DUB) are also parastatals under MPWEM. An organization chart of
the Ministry given is given in Annex 2.   The  itinistry of Commerce and
Transportatiotn supervises petroleum imports and Socit& d'Entreposage de
Pitrole (SEP). which is owned jointly by five foreign companies, operates



petroleum handling  and storage facilities at Bujumbura's  port.   Rural
electrification ts the responsibility of the DNpartement de l'hydraulique
et  de  l'electrification  rurale  (DUER)  under  the  Ministry  of  Rural
Development.
1.07       The international institutions in the sector are mainly related
to the electricity subsector and detailed descriptions may be fouud in
para. 2.04 and Annex 3.
Energy Resources
Woodfuel
1.08      Burundi's forest resources are estimated at about 200,000 ha, and
cover nearly 7Z of the total land area.   About 140,000 ha are planted,
while 60,000 ha are natural forests.  Wood production from natural forest
and plantationt growth is estimated to be below the conservative consumption
estimate of 1 millioLL .3 annually. Bionmass fuels (fuelwood, charcoal, crop
and antimal residues) account for about 952 of the total energy cousumed.
Accurate data on firewood use in rural areas, where 902 of the population
resides, are unavailable.   The estimated average annual wood consumption
per capita is 0.25 m3/year, low by iuternational standards.  Most of the
firewood is collected from   plantations,  farmers' holdings and natural
forests and the volume traded is estimated at about 160,000 m3/year (solid
measurement at 600 kg/m3). Marketed firewood supplies the principal towns
and rural institutions, such as missions and hospitals, and rural
industries, such as tea factories.   Burundi's capital, Bujumbura, with a
population of about 200,000, consumes almost all marketed wood in the form
of charcoal as the principal cooking fuel.
1.09       The fuelwood scarcity has direct impacL on food crop production,
since the rural population is increasingly using agricultural residues to
meet their fuel needs. In some areas, agricultural wastes are the primary
fuel. This reduces soil fertility and crop yields, which in turn increases
pressure on land, and leads to shortened fallow periods, creatiing a cycle
of  soil  degradation  and  falling  production.    The wood  shortage  and
accompanying high prices affect the urban populatiou in that up to 25% of a
typical Bujumbura family's  income is spent ot charcoal.   Prices  have
iniereased dramatically as a consequence of demand pressure on the declining
resource and higher transport costs for charcoal, as trees have been
stripped further and further afield from Bujumbura.   For instance,  the
price increased from BuF 150/bag (US$1.28/bag) of 35 kg in 1970 to
BuF 750/bag (USS6.41/bag) in 1984, i.e. the equivalent of US$180 per toni
atd US$250 per ton of oil equivalent, an itcrease of about 400% compared
with an increase of about 250% in the coLnsumer price iLndex for that period.
1.10            The  possible  substitutes  for charcoal  and  firewood  are
electricity, kerosene and peat.  Electricity for cooking at present tariff
levels costs roughly the same as charcoal (equivalent energy value,
excluding cost of appliance) at present market prices and a few higher
income families are using it, but the high cost of electric stoves and the
conuections are beyond the reach of most of the urban population. The cost
of  imported  petroleum  products  alone   (US$85/bbl  c.i.f  Bujumbura,
(para. 1.18) even excluding the cost of stoves, prohibits using kerosene
for cooklug except for the affluent.   Efforts have been made to supply



-4-
peat as a cooking fuel. but to date it has no advantages over charcoal in
cost or acceptability.   It is clear that woodfuel will remain the main
source of energy for household purposes in the foreseeable future, and
efforts to deal with the wood shortage must focus on increasing the supply
and    the  efficiency  of  use,  given  the  rather  limited  substitution
potential.  However, in the medium and longer term an analysis of the roles
of cooking with electricity, kerosene or charcoal is needed.
1.11        The Forestry Department is under the Ministry of Agriculture.
The Director of Forestry also deals with fisheries and wildlife. Forestry
development has had low priority in budget allocation; protection programs
have until recently been small and ineffective. There is a critical lack
of well-trained extension workers and foresters in Burundi. There is no
trained national forester working in the Forestry Department. The forest
law of Burundi (Code Forestier) has recently been approved and
promulgated. A draft Forestry Policy Review is being prepared, funded by
Fonds European de developpement (FED), for the purpose of defining further
Government forestry objectives and providing a rational framework for
future projects including ones to be financed by external donors.  It is
the first comprehensive document on the subsector.
1.12      Increasing the efficiency of fuelwood utilization in households
is as important an energy issue as increasing fuelwood supplies, given the
inefficient cooking devices and methods which prevail.   Xore efficient
charcoal production methods have been tested and promoted under the IDA
First Forestry Project (CR 918-BU).  Under the Urban Development Project
(CR 1049-BU) an improved charcoal stove was designed which provides fuel
savings of 30Z compared to the traditional stov'. A consultant funded by
ESMAP, has modified the design of the improved stove to make it cheaper for
artisans to produce, and under a two year extension of CR 1049-BU funding
will be provided for a program to promote sales, and to disseminate stoves
(para. 4.15). In contrast to the work on urban charcoal stoves, there has
been little progress on improved woodstoves for rural areas in Burundi.
The rural population accounts for most of the woodfuel consumption, and the
search for methods of increasing the efficiency of utilization must
continue (para. 1.24).
1.13         Various countries and organizations are providing forestry
assistance, including Belgium, Germany, Switzerland, France, the African
Development Bank (AfDB), FED and UNDP. IDA's involvement in the sector is
decribed in para. 1.24.   Technical assistance still falls short of the
need; and better coordination and greater effort are required to decrease
the increasingly serious problems brought about by fuelwood shortages and
deforestation.
Hydroelectricity
1.14      Burundi's mountainous terrain and abundant rainfall result in a
significant potential for the generation of electricity by small
hydroelectric generating stations in the numerous, steep, fast-flowing
rivers and streams. The total usable hydroelectric potential was found by
Lahmeyer International (Federal Republic of Germany) to be 285 MW 3/.
This is about 26 times Burundi's current electricity demand and its
3/ Study of the Development of Hydroelectric Resources in Burundi (Lahmeyer
International), August 1983.



-5-
exploitation is likely to be gradual.   By etd-1984,  ouly 11.7 MW of
installed hydro capacity was developed (para. 2.05).
Peat
1.15       Peat is a potential substitute for firewood for some industries
and for rural institutions. However production problems keep the greater
part of the known resources unavailable or prohibitively expensive. In the
highland peat bogs, about one million tonues of peat are estimated to
exist; a recent report 4/ concludes that this source can meet iudustrial
demand on a competitive basis for a significant period of time and a
strategy for development of the use of peat in under review. About four
million tonnes were recently located at Nyamuswaga; a hydrological survey
is required to determine if and how the bog, which is disadvantageously
distant  from  Bujumbura,  can  be  exploited.    Burundi  has  major  peat
resources, estimated at over 52 million tonnes, in an area on the border
with Rwaanda known as the Akanyaru Basin. The Akanyaru peat is colloidal
and the area is flooded or very swampy throughout the year, making manual
extraction not feasible.   The other available method is hydraulic peat
mining which requires operations on a large scale and thus a substantial
market.  Furthermore, unanswered questions remain about the environmental
effects of large-scale exploitation of these peat resources.   Tt is uot
known whether the bogs can be drained without causing serious damage to the
densely populated farmland downstream, and in parts of R.wanda.
1,16        Consumption of peat to date has been very limited due to the
small amount produced. The Burundi army purchases about 802 of all pear
sold, and the remainder is sold to brlckmaking operations, tea plantations,
bakeries, hospitals, and to missions located near the bog sites. About
10,900 tonnes of peat were produced in 1982, about 13,300 tonues in 1983,
and production is expected to reach 15,000 tonnes ia 1984. However actual
production capability is about 50,000 tonnes annually.
1,17       Peat is currently an unknown as a household fuel.  In spite of
ONATOUR's marketing efforts in Bujumbura, including the free distribution
of a peat cooking stove with the purchose of peat, peat has not gaited
acceptance as a substitute to charcoal -'or household cooking. People are
accustomed to cooking with charcoal and there is no foreseeable
justification for a price incentive to use peat.
Imported Energy: Petroleum
1.18       Burundi is entirely dependent on foreign sources for its supply
of petroleum products and its landlocked location puts it at a particular
disadvantage, with trausport costs of about US$38 per barrel added to the
landed Mombasa cost of petroleum products. The major issues are related to
the very high cost of imported oil and the vulnerability of the supply
routes to interruption. Until 1977, almost all of the oil imports crossed
Tanzania by rail from Dar es Salaam to Kigoma, and were barged up Lake
Tanganyika to Bujumbura. However, rail transportation has been abaudonned
due to floods and tank car shortages resulting from the lack of
4/ Burundi-Peat Utilization Projects - Activity Completion Report - ESMAP;
December 1984.



-6-
maintetiace.   As a result, all of the imported oil is transported from
Mombasa by pipeliae to Nairobi, aLnd by tank truck for a 1,800 kilometer
journey via Uganda and Rwanda. Currently about half of Burundi's petroleum
products are purchased from the Mombasa refinery, and the remainder (since
March  1983)  from  the Middle East  open market.   The price structure
established by the Ministry of Commerce and Industry in May 1983, of about
US$85 per barrel is based on the price of products from the Mombasa
refinery, (i.e. including freight but exclusive of taxes). Total transport
costs i.e. including freight, insurance, and Rwandan fees but excluding any
Burundian duties or taxes from Nombassa to Bujumbura come to about US$242
per tonne of gas oil (18.55 BuFfliter).  Price levels were last adjusted in
December 1983.

1.19          Following a recommendationi by the Energy Assessment mission
(para. 1.03) and at the request of the Government, a consultant, funded by
ESMAP, visited Burundi in August 1983 to assist the Government in reviewing
and evaluating alternative schemes for the importation and distribution of
petroleum products  imi order to devise methods of reducing cost.   The
consultant's review of the possible sources and supply routes confirmed
that, the least-cost alteruative would be to purchase finished products
directly on the Middle East open market, unload them at Dar es Salaam, and
transport them on the Tanzanian railroad to Kigoma and from there by barge
to Bujumbura.   For gasoil, which now accounts for the largest share of
Burundi's petroleum market, this alternative would be about 11 percent less
costly than purchase on. the Middle East open market amd transport via
Kenya Uganda Rwanda. Rowever, the unreliability of the Tanzanian railroad
poses a major  constraint to this option.   For security  reasons,  the
Government is continuing to import petroleum products via Kenya Uganda
Rwanda, while the Tanzanian route is being tested by the oil-importing
companies. A component of the proposed project would be the provision of
technical expertise in the petroleum marketing field, to advise Government
on the least cost method of purchase its petroleum needs, and on the
pricing structure (para. 4.16).
1.20       Burundi imported 38,736 tons of petroleum products in 1982 and
40,489 tons in 1983, an annual increase of about 4.5%. The costs of these
imports accounted for about 16 % of total merchandise imports and 37% of
merchandise exports on a c.i.f. basis in those years.   Following is a
tabulation showing each products's share of the domestic market in 1982 and
1983.

1.21      The product mix of Burundi's oil consumption is heavily weighted
toward vehicle fuels as gasoline and gasoil account for over 90% of the
total.  Stationary engines and small boilers account for a small part of
the demand. The two largest oil consumers are the brewery and the textile
plant, in Bujumbura, where fuel oil is used primarily for process steam.
This is one area where either electricity or peat could be used in place of
oil.
1.22      Burundi's vulnerability to interruptions in delivery via the two
surface routes was demonstrated in the first half of 1979, when the war in
Uganda led to both routes being closed simultaneously.   The possibility
that this situation may recur has led the Government to build additional
storage tanks at Gitega. However, these tanks are empty as the Government
has not completed its studies on the problems of financing and managing
the stocks.  The NPWEM is in the process of drawing up an emergency plan as
was recommended in the Energy Assessment Report.
1.23        The Government received technical assistance, funded through
ES!AP and the IDA Third Technical Assistance Project (CR 917-BU), in
negotiating a petroleum exploration contract with AMOCO (para. 1.02). The
contract was signed in October 1984, is due to be submitted to the National
Assembly and on approval will be promulgated.   Fieldwork will then start
under the direction of AMOCO's local representative.
IDA's Role in the Energy Sector
1.24      The energy assessment of 1981 and the subsequent follow up by the
Government under ESMAP (paras. 1.03 and 1.04) particularly in the petroleum
subsector, have supplied the framework for IDA's proposed or active role in
addressing key energy issues under various projects:
(a) Woodfuel issues: The IDA First Forestry Project (CR 918-BU) is
providing funds for the study of woodfuel consumption, the
introduction on ar operational basis of metal kilns to improve
the efficiency of charcoal production, and the design of
improved woodstoves for rural areas. The project was conceived
as the first stage of a long-term program to develop basic



-8-
poles and timber.  By the tLie of its completion in 1985, it is
expected that the project will have established about 2,000 ha
of eucalyptus (100% of target), 4,000 ha of pine (80X) and 60
rural  nurseries.      Under  the  Urban  Development  Project
(CR 1049-BU), a more efficient-model of the traditional charcoal
stove has been produced (para. 1.12) and continuation of the
program is envisaged under the proposed project (para. 4.15).
The Second Forestry Project, which was appraised in July 1984,
would cover rural nurseries and agroforestry, afforestation
(1,500 ha pine and 5,500 ha eucalyptus), forestry training,
support to agriculture and livestock, and technical assistance
f-r project management and institution building.  The project
would also provide for the training of about 20 charcoal burners
in improved charcoal making. Estimated cost of the project is
about US$17.2 million of which about US$7.5 million is the
portion for foreign currency expenses.
(b) Peat: The Nickel Exploration/Engineering Project (CR 1154-BU)
is helping to finance the Akanyaru Basin trial peat production
(para. 1.15).
Cc) Petroleum: A component of the proposed project is the provision
of funds to cover the cost of a petroleum expert who would
advise on the purchase of petroleum products and a pricing
strategy (para. 4.16).
Cd) Power subsector issues: The Ruzizi II project (CR 1419-BU;
1420-RW; 1421-ZR) is the least-cost option for providing
additional   electricity   to   the   region  and  to   Burundi
(pars. 2.10).   The proposed project is a logical follow-up to
the Ruzizi II project, by financing the needed transmission
facilities to connect the generating plant to the market. The
project also will address improvement of reliability and
efficiency and extension of the transmission and distribution
system-to serve more consumers.
A project preparation advance (PPF P245) for the proposed project
(para. 4.01) has provided funding for feasibility studies of transmission
lines, the electrification of six low-income suburbs of the capital, and an
electricity tariff study.  REGIDESO's training and management needs have
also been studied and will be addressed under the proposed project.
II. THE POWER SUBSECTOR
2.01        The power subsector is controlled by MPWEM (para. 1.06) and the
only utility  is REGIDESO  (para. 2.03).   Rural electrification is the
responsibility of the Departement de l'hydraulique et de l'lelectrification
rurale (DEER) under the Ministry of Rural Development (para. 2.13).
Furthermore, Burundi is a member of two international organizations,
Energie des pays des grands lacs (EGL) and Kagera River Basin Organization
(KBO), which coordinate energy matters on a regional basis (para. 2.04)
and it is a shareholder of the Socieft internationale d'electricite des



-9-
pays des grands lacs (SINELAC) which is constructing the Ruzizi II Regional
Hydroelectric Project (para. 2.10).
Access to Service
2.02        The availability of electric power in Burundi has been, in
general, limited to Bujumbura (95.5% of the sales in 1983), and the
isolated towns of Gitega, Muramvya and Bururi which represented 3.5X; 0.8Z
and 0.2% of the sales, respectively. Only 2% of the population has access
to electricity (among the lowest in Africa), and the average estimated per
capita annual consumption of electricity was about 14 kWh in 1983.
The Power Supply Company
2.03       REGIDESO, an entirely Government-owned organization, was formed
in 1968 to generate, transmit and distribute electricity, and to treat,
pump, and distribute water in Burundi. Responsibility for sanitation was
added in 1979 but was later given to the municipalities. Although it has a
monopoly for the supply of electricity, REGIDESO operates only in the major
centers of the country,  and needs to receive management assistance to
expand and to improve its operations (para. 4.12).  Issues to be addressed
by  this assistance  are mentioned under the appropriate headings.    A
complete description of the organization may be foand beginning in
para. 3.02.
International Institutions
2.04           EGL, formed in 1974 by Burundi,  Rwanda and Zaire, with
responsibility for planning the least-cost method of providing electricity
to the Great Lakes Region, was instrumental in the preparation of the
Ruzizi II project.  EGL reports to the Com-inaute economique des pays des
grands lacs (CEPGL) formed by the three countries.  Since the creation of
SINELAC in February 1984, EGL transferred its responsibilities for
Ruzizi II to this company and is presently charged with identifying and
studying other energy resources.   KBO, which represents Burundi, Rwanda,
Tanzania and Uganda, has had several reports prepared by consultants on the
development of the Kagera river, which has its source in Burundi, and forms
the border between Rwanda and Tanzania.   A description of these three
organizations may be found in Annex 3.
Existing Generating Facilities
2.05         The t-otal installed capacity in Burundi is 19.8 MW of which
11.7 MW (60%) is hydro anD the remaining 8.1 MW (40Z) is diesel.  In 1983
supply from diesels was less than 1% of the total output. REGIDESO has a
total installed generating capacity of 16.8 MW, of which 10.7 MW (64%) is
hydro and the remainder diesel. The Mugere generating station (4 x 2 MW),
funded and constructed by the Chinese Government, provides electric power
to Bujuubura, the main demand center.  This is a run-of-river plant with a
maximum available capacity of 7.5 MW, but because of the lack of a storage
reservoir, its output during the dry season (July, August and September)
may be reduced to about 2 MW; the annual generation capability of the plant
is 41 GWh.   The diesel generating station in Bujumbura has an installed
capacity of 5.5 MW but due to age and lack of maintenance its maxifum



- 10 -
output cannot exceed about 2.0 MW, and it is kept only as a standby. There
are also many small hydro and diesel plants installed at isolated missions,
farms, hospitals and tea estates.  A list of all generating facilities in
the country, summarized below, may be found in Annex 4, and those owned by
REGIDESO are shown on IBRD Hap 18517.

Transmission and Distribution
2.07       Electric power from the Buzizi generating station is supplied to
Bujuinbura via a 70 kV transmission line 115 km long, (owned and operated by
S.N.EL.)  and  from the Mugere  generating station via a  25 km,  35 kV
transmission line.   There  is no interconnection  between  the receiving
substations, and, because of the different voltage levels, the output of
the two plants cannot be synchronized.   As a result, the Mugere plant
operates in isolation, and since commissioning supplies only a section of
Bujumbura, previously supplied from the Ruzizi I station.   This lack of
operating flexibility will be corrected upon completion of part of a 30 kV
ring around Bujumbura now under construction (Map IBRD 18516).   Most of
Bujumbura is supplied Arom the S.N.EL.substation located at the termination
of the 70 kV Ruzizi I Bujumbura line, where two transformers of 5.25 MVA
each are installed and third similar transformer is available as a reserve
but not connected.  The capacity of the line is about 9.5 KW and it was
reached in 1981. The substation, which has been surrounded by residential
districts,  is also owned and operated by S.N.EL.   The energy from the
Ruzizi I power plant is metered at this substation.   The line and the
substation were inspected by consultants to S.N.EL. within the feasibility
studies of the proposed Zaire Power II Project (para. 2.30).  The 70 kV
transmission line does not require extensive rehabilitation and equipment
and works needed for the rehabilitation of the Bujumbura substation have
been estimated about US$0.3 million (para. 4.07).  The highest voltage at



- 12 -
which electricity is transmitted by REGIDESO is 30 kV, and there are only
about 36 km of line at this voltage.  A 15 kV transmission line, owned by
S.N.EL., supplies power to Uvira in Zaire from the Bujumbura substation
(IBRD Map 18517).   Gitega, Bururi and Muramvya are supplied from small
hydro stations built in the vicinity of these towns. The total length of
the transmission and distribution lines which, at the end of 1983, was
423.3 km was made up as follows:

Electricity is distributed to domestic and commercial consumers within

the  consumer  at  400/230  V.    Distribution  by  underground  cables  is
expensive, and aerial lines are now used in the new areas.

Average annual consumption of the medium and low voltage consumers in 1983
were 300,000 kWh and 4,157 kWh respectively. 5/ In 1983, losses were only
2.5% of the total supply. This i8 due to the fact that energy supplied to
Bujumbura (95% of the total) is metered at the S.N.EL. substation iii
Bujumbura and the distribution in the capital is concentrated to the port,
new industrial and high-income residential areas. In the other areas with

and distribution networks are limited. However, with the expansion of the

attain levels of about 12 to 15%.   Therefore ant item in the terms of

unmetered connecetions) basis (paras. 4.12 and 4.26).
Works in Progress
2.09       Until 1982 (para. 2.06) Burundi imported almost all its electric
power from Ruzizi I, located in Zaire. To ensure that at least a strategic
portiom of its weeds are under its coutrol, in 1981 the Government embarked
on the construction of the Rwegura dam and hydroelectric generating station
(3x6 KW) in the northern part of the country, on the Kitenge River 6/
including a 110 kV transmission line to transmit power to Bujumbura.  Th'e
,l;.e will end at the RN1 station in the northeast of Bujumbura. A system
control center for Burundi is also being constructed at this substation and
communication  equipment  is  provided  within  the  project.    ELngineering
services for the projects are carried out by Lahmeyer International.  IDA
was requested to consider providing part of the funds required for the
financing of the Rwegura hydroelectric project, and a mission visited
Burudi in May 1981 to appraise the project. Following appraisal, it was
fouud that the Ruzizi II hydroelectric power geceration project, just
downstream from the existing plant, would be the least cost solution for
the provision of electrical energy to Burundi aud to the region, and IDA
therefore withdrew its interest in Rwegura. Nevertheless, the Government
obtained the aecessary financing from other lending agencies and donors and
REGIDESO is constructing this project finauced by Kreditanstalt fur
Wiederaufbau (KfW), Caisse centrale de cooperation &conomique (CCCE), Fonds
europeen de developpement (FED), African Development Bank (AfDB) and the
Kuwait Fund, with commissioning scheduled for the end of 1986.
2.10        Although Burundi lacks a comprehensive power development plan,
the Government and REGIDESO concluded that it would not be capable of
meeting the electricity demand by 1989, and joiued with Rwanda and Zaire to
develop Ruzizi II on the Rwanda-Zaire border.   In December 1983,  IDA
approved Credit 1419-BU in the amount of US$15 million to Burundi, and
similar amounts to Rwanda (CR 1420-RW) and Zaire (CR 1421-ZR) totaling
US$45 million, to provide part of the financing for the construction of the
Ruzizi II dam (a concrete gravity dam 11 a high) and generating station
(two 13.3 MW units, with provision for a third similar unit) and related
facilities.   Construction activities are now underway with commissioning
expected in mid-1988.  A new international company, Socitre internationale

d'6lectricite des pays des grands lacs (SINELAC) owned in equal shares by

The Credits were declared effective on August 30, 1984.
2.11       FED is financing the construction of a wood-pole 30 kV tratLmis-
sion line, from the site of the Rwegura generating station, to the village
of Rwegura, then to Kayauza, and onwards to Ngozi, estimated at US$1.3
million; a 30 kV line from Bujumbura to Jenda, Tora, Mwaro and Kibumbu at a
cost of US$2.0 million; both are scheduled to be completed in 1985; and the
Bururi-Rumonge hydro facility scheduled to be completed in 1988.
2.12          In addition, DER lis constructing two mini-hydro stations,
totalling only 165 kW and expected to be put into operation by 1986.
Rural Electrification
2.13        Rural electrification is the responsibility of DHER which was
created in 1979. Since then, it has successfully brought into operation 5
mini-hydro generating stations (0.05 to 0.24 MW), two diesel geuerating
stations (both 0.09 MW), and about 27 km of subtransmission and
distribution lines. Two uew generating stations are under construction as
well as about 16 km of transmission lines.  The construction of three more
mini-hydro generating stations which studies indicate to be feasible, are
planned for the immediate future.  Details of the foregoing projects may be
found in Annex 5.
2.14        There is little coordiruation betweenL DRER and REGIDESO.  The
selection of rural  sites,  and the order of  priority  is, in general,
determiued by the lending agency involved.   Also, upon completion,  the
operation, maintenance, and financial obligations are left to the
tec.'pient, i.e., hospital, school, or local government agency with the
result  that  the  maintenance  is  sometimes  inadequate,  and  funds  for
replacement parts are not available.   More appropriately, responsibility
for these projects should rest with REGIDESO, which has the technical
cuapetence to ensure successful operation, and the mnmagerial and financial
capability to integrate these uew projects into the broad, least-cost
development platning of the electrification of the country as a whole. One
of the responsibilities of the management assistance team funded by this
project, would be to determine the best course of action to be taken for
these rural developments (para. 4.12).
Historical Demand for Electric Power
2.15       Demand for electrical energy in Burundi increased by 9% per year
between 1974 aud 1983, from 23.6 GWh to 57.8 GWh, despite the fact that in
1979 the growth in demaud for electrical energy was only 5% over the
previous year, due to the hostilities between Tanzania and Uganda which
interrupted transportation facilities, and significantly reduced commercial



- 15 -
activities.  Since then, however, the average annual growth in demand has
been about 13%.  The greatest itcrease in demand has been from industry
which reached 172 annually in 1980 aud 1981, and 43% in 1983. This latest
increase was due primarily to the commissioning of a glass bottle
manufacturing plant in September 1983, which alone is expected to use about
15 GWh annually.   Growth in the domestic sector has averaged about 11X
annually since 1979, and the demand by public buildings, street lighting
and REGIDESO's owt needs for pumpitng water has averaged about 4X annually.

2.16             The  variation  in  demand  for  electrical  power  in  the
interconnected system serving Bujumbura during a typical working day may be
fouud in Annex 7.
Load Forecasts
2.17       Two forecasts of the demand for electrical energy have been made
recently.   The first in August 1983, by Lahmeyer (para.  1.14) where in
their average estimate indicated that the demand in Bujumbura would rise
from 58 GWh in 1983 to 102 GWh in 1990, an average annual growth rate of



- 16 -
about 8.4%.   Proposed extensions to the interconnected grid would add
another 27 GWh making a total interconnected system demand of 129 GWh, in
1990 (average annual rate, 12%). The most recent forecast September 1984,
by EdF (para. 2.21) was much more optimistic, and forecast that industry
requiring significant amounts of electrical energy would be constructed in
Burundi, raising the demand for electrical energy In the interconnected
system to 122 GWh in 1990.  EdF also estimated that an additional 50 GWh
from  isolated areas woull be connected  to  the grid during  the period
bringing the total demand to 172 GWh in 1990 (average annual rate, 16.8%).
IDA believes that EdF is too optimistic, particularly forecasting future
industrial loads. Following review of both forecasts, REGIDESO's transmis-
sion and distribution expansion program, and further analysis, IDA prepared
its own forecast, which is about the same order with the Lahmeyer fore-
cast. Thus IDA believes that a demand Zor electrical energy of 98 GWh for
Bujumbura and additional connected load of 25 GWh from extension of the
grid to the outlying areas resulting in a total interconnected load of
123 GWh in 1990 is a more reasonable assumption, resulting in an average
annual rate of 11.3%.  Results are sumnarized in the following table and
details are shown in Annex 8.
Summary of Load Forecasts - Interconnected System

Total sales of REGIDESO, including isolated areas, would increase from
74 GWh in 1985 to 129 GCh in 1990 at an average annual rate of 11.7%.
Regional Interconnected System
2.18       In examining the electric energy needs of Burundi, it is neces-
sary to take the demand for Rwanda and the Kivu province of Zaire into con-
sideration as well, because all three countries are interconnected and
depend on the original Ruzizi I generating station at the outlets of Lwake
Kivu for part of their supply.  This interdependence is being strengthened
with the construction of Rauzizi II.   A forecast of the availability of
supply and demand for electrical energy in the regional interconnected
system is given in Annex 9 and summarized in the table on the following
page.
2.19       The demand forecast for the interconnected system is equivalent
to an average  annual  increase  in demand of  10% during   he  1984-1995
period. Review of these supply and demand forecasts leads to the following
conclusions:
A. From the regional (Burundi-Rwanda-Zaire/Kivu) point of view:
(a) Energy from the Ruzizi II project would be required beginning in
1989 to supply the regional needs; as the present commissioning

B. From the point of view of Burundi power subsector:
(a) Following  commissioning  of  the  Rwegura  and  Ruzizi  II  power
stations, Burundi is expected to bave an oversupply of electric
power from the interconnected system unti;. end-1993; however
without Rwegura, it would not have been possible to meet daily
peak demands and the energy requirements from 1987 onwards,
until the Ruzizi II Project is coumissioned; and,
(b) Burundi will start using its share of Ruzizi II in 1988
(para. 2.21); and will need to use more than lts share in 1994.



- 18 -
2.20       The ability of REGIDESO to absorb this surplus by extending the
interconnected grid to other regional towns is limited by the availability
of capital, bo h in foreign and local currency, and the viability of the
demand.   The proposed project would provide funds  to construct 30 kV
subtransmission   lines   and   distribution   networks   to   extend   the
interconnected grid.
Need for the Project
2.21       Based on the mission's forecast of demand, which is the lowest
of the three forecasts (para. 2.17), it would be necessary to provide
Burundi with power from the Ruzizi II project in 1988, in order to prevent
any possible shortfall, which could arise over demand higher than forecast
or adverse hydrologic conditions which could limit the generation from
Mugere and Ruegura power stations, particularly in the dry months of July,
August and September.  Costs expected to be incurred (fuel and shortage
costs) in 1989 in case the Ruzizi II Burundi transmission link is delayed,
demand is higher than forecast and/or hydrogeneration is lower than
expected, were found to exceed the benefits to be gained by delaying the
line by one year. Therefore, it is justified to have the transmission link
in operation in 1989.  Early in 1984, REGIDESO retained the services of
consultants to carry out a detailed study to determine the least-cost
method of transmitting the Ruzizi II energy to Burundi. The consultants
(EdF) have found that the least costly method of bringing this energy to
Bujumbura was to construct a 110 kV transmission line (83 km) from
Ruzizi II to Bubanza, where it would be joined to the 110 kV Rwegura
Bujumbura line now under construction.  The commissioning of the Rwegura
generating station and its transmission line is scheduled before the end of
1986.
2.22      The foregoing concerns the requirements to meet the energy needs,
but REGIDESO will have difficulty meeting the daily peak demand in 1986.
The capacity of the existing 70 kV line from Ruzizi is about 9.5 MW and
about 2.5 MW could be generated in the diesel station. Thus, with about a
maximum of 2 MW out of Mugere during the dry season, after 1985 when the
peak is expected to exceed i4 MW, it will be necessary for REGIDESO to
institute a load shedding program. This will no longer be a problem after
the Rwegura generating station (18 MW) is commissioned in late 1986.
2.23        In accordance with the Government's strategy (para. 2.28) to
extend the availability of electricity to as yet unserved markets, the
consultants also studied the possibility of extending the system to provide
service to potential new consumers in the areas and small towns surrounding
Cibitoke and Bubanza and found that these extensions would be justified
(para. 6.07).   The provision of electrical services to six low-income
districts of the capital was also studied and found justified (para. 6.10).
Long-Term Power Development Plan
2.24         In the past, Burundi did not have a plan which ranked the
various alternative sources of its potential hydro sites in order of
priority.   As a result of the anticipated growth  in  the demand  for
electrical energy, the Guvernment engaged Lahmeyer International of
Frankfurt, Germany (funded by FED in 1983) to carry out a comprehensive
study of the hydroelectric resources of Burundi.   Lahmeyer identified



- 19 -
94 potential sites, and after prellminaty investigation, reduced the number
which could be considered as potential sources of power to 41, with a total
output of 285 KW, capable of generating about 1,130 GWh annually.
2.25      Lahmeyer, studied the demand for electrical energy until the year
2005, and prepared three forecasts of load growth (para. 2.17): optimistic,
pessimistic and average.   The recommended development schedule for all
three cases was as following:

The Government is reviewing the report and has not started work on the
planned projects.
2.26       However, the Lahmeyer study was limited to Burundi, and did not
take into consideration the possibility of further regional development
which may be more attractive when the needs of Rwanda and the Kivu region
of Zaire are also considered. IDA drew the Government's attention to this
omission and subsequently discussed this subject with EGL  (para.  2.04)
during the appraisal and as a result EGL requested appropriate funds from
FED and the Governments of Italy and Federal Republic of Germany to carry
out further studies covering the total needs of the Great Lakes Region.
FED funded the studies leading to the Ruzizi II proiect and, in order to
ensure that the development of electric power facilities in the area is
carried out in accordance with a least-cost development plan, has indicated
that it is interested in funding another study covering regional needs.
IDA is of the opinion that power supply should be considered on a regional
basis and therefore, if FED or the Governments of Italy or Federal Republic
of Germany are unable to allocate funds, EGL should try other international
resources, such as UNDP, or IDA would give consideration to the possibility
of an engineering credit to EGL with the guarantee of the three Governments
for this study to be carried out during 1986. The proposed timetable for
the studies and implementation of the regional project is given in
Annex 9.  IDA would be given the opportunity to comment on the terms of
reference, review and comment on the recommendations, and seek an agreement
with the governments on a long-term power generation plan, based on the
findings of the study.
